     <p><b>Genetic engineering</b>, also called <b>genetic modification</b>, is the direct manipulation of an organism's <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> using <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a>. It is a set of technologies used to change the genetic makeup of cells, including the transfer of genes within and across species boundaries to produce improved or novel <a href="/wiki/Organisms" title="Organisms" class="mw-redirect">organisms</a>. New <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> may be inserted in the host genome by first isolating and copying the genetic material of interest using <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloning" title="Molecular cloning">molecular cloning</a> methods to generate a DNA sequence, or by synthesizing the DNA, and then inserting this construct into the host organism. <a href="/wiki/Genes" title="Genes" class="mw-redirect">Genes</a> may be removed, or "knocked out", using a <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nuclease</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gene_targeting" title="Gene targeting">Gene targeting</a> is a different technique that uses <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombination</a> to change an endogenous gene, and can be used to delete a gene, remove <a href="/wiki/Exons" title="Exons" class="mw-redirect">exons</a>, add a gene, or introduce <a href="/wiki/Point_mutation" title="Point mutation">point mutations</a>.</p> 
     <p>An organism that is generated through genetic engineering is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organism</a> (GMO). The first GMOs were <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> generated in 1973 and GM mice in 1974. <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">Insulin</a>-producing bacteria were commercialized in 1982 and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a> has been sold since 1994. <a href="/wiki/GloFish" title="GloFish">GloFish</a>, the first GMO designed as a pet, was first sold in the United States in December 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Genetic engineering techniques have been applied in numerous fields including research, agriculture, industrial biotechnology, and medicine. Enzymes used in laundry detergent and medicines such as insulin and human growth hormone are now manufactured in GM cells, experimental GM cell lines and GM animals such as mice or <a href="/wiki/Zebrafish" title="Zebrafish">zebrafish</a> are being used for research purposes, and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a> have been commercialized.</p> 
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"> 
      <div class="" style="background-color: #F9F9F9; color: black; text-align: center; font-size: larger; font-weight: bold;">
       IUPAC definition
      </div> 
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;"> 
       <div> 
        <p>Process of inserting new genetic information into existing cells in order to<br> modify a specific organism for the purpose of changing its characteristics.</p> 
        <p><i>Note</i>: Adapted from ref.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p> 
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p></p>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/300px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png" width="300" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/450px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg/600px-Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1077" data-file-height="921"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Breeding_transgenesis_cisgenesis.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Comparison of conventional plant breeding with transgenic and cisgenic genetic modification.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Genetic engineering alters the genetic make-up of an organism using techniques that remove <a href="/wiki/Heritable" title="Heritable" class="mw-redirect">heritable</a> material or that introduce DNA prepared outside the organism either directly into the host or into a cell that is then <a href="/wiki/Cell_fusion" title="Cell fusion">fused</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridized</a> with the host.<sup id="cite_ref-EU17_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU17-4">[4]</a></sup> This involves using <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant nucleic acid</a> (<a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> or <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>) techniques to form new combinations of heritable genetic material followed by the incorporation of that material either indirectly through a <a href="/wiki/Vector_(molecular_biology)" title="Vector (molecular biology)">vector</a> system or directly through <a href="/wiki/Microinjection" title="Microinjection">micro-injection</a>, macro-injection and <a href="/wiki/Micro-encapsulation" title="Micro-encapsulation">micro-encapsulation</a> techniques.</p> 
     <p>Genetic engineering does not normally include traditional <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">plant breeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilisation" title="In vitro fertilisation">in vitro fertilisation</a>, induction of <a href="/wiki/Polyploidy" title="Polyploidy" class="mw-redirect">polyploidy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutagenesis" title="Mutagenesis">mutagenesis</a> and cell fusion techniques that do not use recombinant nucleic acids or a genetically modified organism in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-EU17_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EU17-4">[4]</a></sup> However the European Commission has also defined genetic engineering broadly as including <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> and other means of artificial selection.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">Cloning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> research, although not considered genetic engineering,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> are closely related and genetic engineering can be used within them.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">Synthetic biology</a> is an emerging discipline that takes genetic engineering a step further by introducing artificially synthesized material from raw materials into an organism.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>If genetic material from another species is added to the host, the resulting organism is called <a href="/wiki/Transgenic" title="Transgenic" class="mw-redirect">transgenic</a>. If genetic material from the same species or a species that can naturally breed with the host is used the resulting organism is called <a href="/wiki/Cisgenic" title="Cisgenic" class="mw-redirect">cisgenic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Genetic engineering can also be used to remove genetic material from the target organism, creating a <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">gene knockout</a> organism.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In Europe genetic modification is <a href="/wiki/Synonymous" title="Synonymous" class="mw-redirect">synonymous</a> with genetic engineering while within the United States of America it can also refer to conventional breeding methods.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The Canadian regulatory system is based on whether a product has novel features regardless of method of origin. In other words, a product is regulated as genetically modified if it carries some trait not previously found in the species whether it was generated using traditional breeding methods (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_fusion" title="Cell fusion">cell fusion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mutation_breeding" title="Mutation breeding">mutation breeding</a>) or genetic engineering.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Within the scientific community, the term <i>genetic engineering</i> is not commonly used; more specific terms such as <i>transgenic</i> are preferred.</p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetically_modified_organisms">Genetically modified organisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Genetically modified organisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organism</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Plants, animals or micro organisms that have changed through genetic engineering are termed genetically modified organisms or GMOs.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">Bacteria</a> were the first organisms to be genetically modified. Plasmid DNA containing new genes can be inserted into the bacterial cell and the bacteria will then express those genes. These genes can code for medicines or enzymes that process food and other <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_substrate_(biology)" title="Enzyme substrate (biology)" class="mw-redirect">substrates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Plants have been modified for insect protection, herbicide resistance, virus resistance, enhanced nutrition, tolerance to environmental pressures and the production of edible vaccines.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Most commercialised GMO's are insect resistant and/or herbicide tolerant crop plants.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Genetically modified animals have been used for research, model animals and the production of agricultural or pharmaceutical products.</p> 
     <p>The genetically modified animals include animals with <a href="/wiki/Knock-out_mice" title="Knock-out mice" class="mw-redirect">genes knocked out</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oncomouse" title="Oncomouse">increased susceptibility to disease</a>, hormones for extra growth and the ability to express proteins in their milk.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_genetic_engineering" title="History of genetic engineering">History of genetic engineering</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Humans have altered the genomes of species for thousands of years through <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>, or artificial selection<sup id="cite_ref-Root_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Root-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zohary_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zohary-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:1</sup> as contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, and more recently through <a href="/wiki/Mutagenesis" title="Mutagenesis">mutagenesis</a>. Genetic engineering as the direct manipulation of DNA by humans outside breeding and mutations has only existed since the 1970s. The term "genetic engineering" was first coined by <a href="/wiki/Jack_Williamson" title="Jack Williamson">Jack Williamson</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_novel" title="Science fiction novel" class="mw-redirect">science fiction novel</a> <i>Dragon's Island</i>, published in 1951<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> – one year before DNA's role in <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a> was confirmed by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hershey" title="Alfred Hershey">Alfred Hershey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martha_Chase" title="Martha Chase">Martha Chase</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and two years before <a href="/wiki/James_D._Watson" title="James D. Watson" class="mw-redirect">James Watson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> showed that the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> molecule has a double-helix structure – though the general concept of direct genetic manipulation was explored in rudimentary form in <a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Weinbaum" title="Stanley G. Weinbaum">Stanley G. Weinbaum</a>'s 1936 science fiction story <i>Proteus Island</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg/220px-Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg/330px-Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg/440px-Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1800"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Jaenisch_2003_by_Sam_Ogden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> In 1974 
        <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Jaenisch" title="Rudolf Jaenisch">Rudolf Jaenisch</a> created the first 
        <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_mouse" title="Genetically modified mouse">GM animal</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>In 1972, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Berg" title="Paul Berg">Paul Berg</a> created the first <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant DNA</a> molecules by combining DNA from the monkey virus <a href="/wiki/SV40" title="SV40">SV40</a> with that of the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_phage" title="Lambda phage">lambda virus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1973 <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Boyer" title="Herbert Boyer">Herbert Boyer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cohen_(biochemist)" title="Stanley Cohen (biochemist)">Stanley Cohen</a> created the first <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_organism" title="Transgenic organism" class="mw-redirect">transgenic organism</a> by inserting <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance#Applications" title="Antibiotic resistance" class="mw-redirect">antibiotic resistance genes</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> of an <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> bacterium.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> A year later <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Jaenisch" title="Rudolf Jaenisch">Rudolf Jaenisch</a> created a <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_mouse" title="Transgenic mouse" class="mw-redirect">transgenic mouse</a> by introducing foreign DNA into its embryo, making it the world’s first <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_animal" title="Transgenic animal" class="mw-redirect">transgenic animal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> These achievements led to concerns in the scientific community about potential risks from genetic engineering, which were first discussed in depth at the <a href="/wiki/Asilomar_conference_on_recombinant_DNA" title="Asilomar conference on recombinant DNA" class="mw-redirect">Asilomar Conference</a> in 1975. One of the main recommendations from this meeting was that government oversight of recombinant DNA research should be established until the technology was deemed safe.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 1976 <a href="/wiki/Genentech" title="Genentech">Genentech</a>, the first genetic engineering company, was founded by Herbert Boyer and <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Swanson" title="Robert A. Swanson">Robert Swanson</a> and a year later the company produced a human protein (<a href="/wiki/Somatostatin" title="Somatostatin">somatostatin</a>) in <i>E.coli</i>. Genentech announced the production of genetically engineered human <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Supreme_Court" title="U.S. Supreme Court" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Supreme Court</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_v._Chakrabarty" title="Diamond v. Chakrabarty">Diamond v. Chakrabarty</a></i> case ruled that genetically altered life could be patented.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The insulin produced by bacteria, branded <a href="/wiki/Humulin" title="Humulin" class="mw-redirect">humulin</a>, was approved for release by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In the 1970s graduate student Steven Lindow of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison" title="University of Wisconsin–Madison">University of Wisconsin–Madison</a> with D.C. Arny and C. Upper found a <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a> he identified as <a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_syringae" title="Pseudomonas syringae"><i>P. syringae</i></a> that played a role in ice nucleation, and in 1977 he discovered a mutant <a href="/wiki/Ice-minus_bacteria" title="Ice-minus bacteria">ice-minus</a> strain. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://pmb.berkeley.edu/profile/slindow">Dr. Lindow</a> (who is now a plant pathologist at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California-Berkeley</a>) later successfully created a recombinant ice-minus strain.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In 1983, a biotech company, Advanced Genetic Sciences (AGS) applied for U.S. government authorization to perform field tests with the ice-minus strain of <i>P. syringae</i> to protect crops from frost, but environmental groups and protestors delayed the field tests for four years with legal challenges.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1987, the ice-minus strain of <i>P. syringae</i> became the first <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organism (GMO)</a> to be released into the environment<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2002_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2002-39">[39]</a></sup> when a strawberry field and a potato field in California were sprayed with it.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Both test fields were attacked by activist groups the night before the tests occurred: "The world's first trial site attracted the world's first field trasher".<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2002_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2002-39">[39]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The first field trials of <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_plants" title="Transgenic plants" class="mw-redirect">genetically engineered plants</a> occurred in France and the USA in 1986, tobacco plants were engineered to be resistant to <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The People’s Republic of China was the first country to commercialize transgenic plants, introducing a virus-resistant tobacco in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-James1997_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James1997-42">[42]</a></sup> In 1994 <a href="/wiki/Monsanto#Spin-offs_and_mergers" title="Monsanto">Calgene</a> attained approval to commercially release the <a href="/wiki/Flavr_Savr" title="Flavr Savr">Flavr Savr</a> tomato, a tomato engineered to have a longer shelf life.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1994, the European Union approved tobacco engineered to be resistant to the herbicide <a href="/wiki/Bromoxynil" title="Bromoxynil">bromoxynil</a>, making it the first genetically engineered crop commercialized in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_potato#NewLeaf" title="Genetically modified potato">Bt Potato</a> was approved safe by the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Environmental Protection Agency" class="mw-redirect">Environmental Protection Agency</a>, after having been approved by the FDA, making it the first pesticide producing crop to be approved in the USA.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In 2009 11 transgenic crops were grown commercially in 25 countries, the largest of which by area grown were the USA, Brazil, Argentina, India, Canada, China, Paraguay and South Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In 2010, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/J._Craig_Venter_Institute" title="J. Craig Venter Institute">J. Craig Venter Institute</a> created the first <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_life" title="Synthetic life" class="mw-redirect">synthetic life</a> form by adding a synthetic <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> to an empty bacterial cell. The resulting bacterium was named <a href="/wiki/Synthia" title="Synthia" class="mw-redirect">Synthia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In 2014, a bacterium was developed that replicated a <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> containing a unique <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pair</a>, creating the first organism engineered to use an expanded genetic alphabet.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Process">Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering_techniques" title="Genetic engineering techniques">Genetic engineering techniques</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The first step is to choose and isolate the gene that will be inserted into the genetically modified organism. As of 2012, most commercialised GM plants have genes transferred into them that provide protection against insects or tolerance to herbicides.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The gene can be isolated using <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzymes" title="Restriction enzymes" class="mw-redirect">restriction enzymes</a> to cut DNA into fragments and <a href="/wiki/Gel_electrophoresis" title="Gel electrophoresis">gel electrophoresis</a> to separate them out according to length.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">Polymerase chain reaction</a> (PCR) can also be used to amplify up a gene segment, which can then be isolated through gel electrophoresis.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> If the chosen gene or the donor organism's <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> has been well studied it may be present in a <a href="/wiki/Library_(biology)" title="Library (biology)">genetic library</a>. If the <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" title="DNA sequence" class="mw-redirect">DNA sequence</a> is known, but no copies of the gene are available, it can be <a href="/wiki/Gene_synthesis" title="Gene synthesis" class="mw-redirect">artificially synthesized</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The gene to be inserted into the genetically modified organism must be combined with other genetic elements in order for it to work properly. The gene can also be modified at this stage for better expression or effectiveness. As well as the gene to be inserted most <a href="/wiki/DNA_construct" title="DNA construct">constructs</a> contain a <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)" class="mw-redirect">promoter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terminator_(genetics)" title="Terminator (genetics)">terminator</a> region as well as a <a href="/wiki/Selectable_marker" title="Selectable marker">selectable marker</a> gene. The promoter region initiates <a href="/wiki/Transcription_(genetics)" title="Transcription (genetics)">transcription</a> of the gene and can be used to control the location and level of gene expression, while the terminator region ends transcription. The selectable marker, which in most cases confers <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance" class="mw-redirect">antibiotic resistance</a> to the organism it is expressed in, is needed to determine which cells are transformed with the new gene. The constructs are made using <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant DNA</a> techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Restriction_digest" title="Restriction digest">restriction digests</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA_ligase" title="DNA ligase">ligations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloning" title="Molecular cloning">molecular cloning</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The manipulation of the DNA generally occurs within a <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a>.</p> 
     <p>The most common form of genetic engineering involves inserting new genetic material randomly within the host genome.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other techniques allow new genetic material to be inserted at a specific location in the host genome or generate mutations at desired <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">genomic loci</a> capable of <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">knocking out</a> <a href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous" class="mw-redirect">endogenous</a> genes. The technique of <a href="/wiki/Gene_targeting" title="Gene targeting">gene targeting</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombination</a> to target desired changes to a specific <a href="/wiki/Endogenous" title="Endogenous" class="mw-redirect">endogenous</a> gene. This tends to occur at a relatively low frequency in plants and animals and generally requires the use of <a href="/wiki/Selectable_markers" title="Selectable markers" class="mw-redirect">selectable markers</a>. The frequency of gene targeting can be greatly enhanced with the use of engineered <a href="/wiki/Nucleases" title="Nucleases" class="mw-redirect">nucleases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger_nucleases" title="Zinc finger nucleases" class="mw-redirect">zinc finger nucleases</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> engineered <a href="/wiki/Homing_endonucleases" title="Homing endonucleases" class="mw-redirect">homing endonucleases</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> or nucleases created from <a href="/wiki/TAL_effector" title="TAL effector">TAL effectors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In addition to enhancing gene targeting, engineered nucleases can also be used to introduce mutations at endogenous genes that generate a <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">gene knockout</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transformation">Transformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Transformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Transformation_(genetics)" title="Transformation (genetics)">Transformation (genetics)</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:189px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Agrobacterium-tumefaciens.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ae/Agrobacterium-tumefaciens.png" width="187" height="237" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="187" data-file-height="237"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Agrobacterium-tumefaciens.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <i><a href="/wiki/A._tumefaciens" title="A. tumefaciens" class="mw-redirect">A. tumefaciens</a></i> attaching itself to a carrot cell
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Only about 1% of bacteria are naturally capable of <a href="/wiki/Competence_(biology)" title="Competence (biology)" class="mw-redirect">taking up foreign DNA</a>. However, this ability can be induced in other bacteria via stress (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Heat_shock" title="Heat shock">thermal</a> or electric shock), thereby increasing the cell membrane's permeability to DNA; up-taken DNA can either integrate with the genome or exist as <a href="/wiki/Extrachromosomal_DNA" title="Extrachromosomal DNA">extrachromosomal DNA</a>. DNA is generally inserted into animal cells using <a href="/wiki/Microinjection" title="Microinjection">microinjection</a>, where it can be injected through the cell's <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_envelope" title="Nuclear envelope" class="mw-redirect">nuclear envelope</a> directly into the <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a> or through the use of <a href="/wiki/Viral_vectors" title="Viral vectors" class="mw-redirect">viral vectors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In plants the DNA is generally inserted using <a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium#Uses_in_biotechnology" title="Agrobacterium"><i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated recombination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biolistics" title="Biolistics" class="mw-redirect">biolistics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>In <i>Agrobacterium</i>-mediated recombination, the <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> construct contains <a href="/wiki/Transfer_DNA" title="Transfer DNA">T-DNA</a>, DNA which is responsible for insertion of the DNA into the host plants genome. This plasmid is transformed into <i>Agrobacterium</i> containing no plasmids prior to infecting the plant cells. The <i>Agrobacterium</i> will then naturally insert the genetic material into the plant cells.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In biolistics transformation particles of gold or tungsten are coated with DNA and then shot into young plant cells or plant embryos. Some genetic material will enter the cells and transform them. This method can be used on plants that are not susceptible to <i>Agrobacterium</i> infection and also allows transformation of plant plastids. Another transformation method for plant and animal cells is <a href="/wiki/Electroporation" title="Electroporation">electroporation</a>. Electroporation involves subjecting the plant or animal cell to an electric shock, which can make the cell membrane permeable to plasmid DNA. In some cases the electroporated cells will incorporate the DNA into their genome. Due to the damage caused to the cells and DNA the transformation efficiency of biolistics and electroporation is lower than agrobacterial mediated transformation and microinjection.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>As often only a single cell is transformed with genetic material the organism must be regenerated from that single cell. As bacteria consist of a single cell and reproduce clonally regeneration is not necessary. In plants this is accomplished through the use of <a href="/wiki/Plant_tissue_culture" title="Plant tissue culture">tissue culture</a>. Each plant species has different requirements for successful regeneration through tissue culture. If successful an adult plant is produced that contains the <a href="/wiki/Transgene" title="Transgene">transgene</a> in every cell. In animals it is necessary to ensure that the inserted DNA is present in the <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cells" title="Embryonic stem cells" class="mw-redirect">embryonic stem cells</a>. <a href="/wiki/Selectable_markers" title="Selectable markers" class="mw-redirect">Selectable markers</a> are used to easily differentiate transformed from untransformed cells. These markers are usually present in the transgenic organism, although a number of strategies have been developed that can remove the selectable marker from the mature transgenic plant.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> When the offspring is produced they can be screened for the presence of the gene. All offspring from the first generation will be <a href="/wiki/Heterozygous" title="Heterozygous" class="mw-redirect">heterozygous</a> for the inserted gene and must be mated together to produce a <a href="/wiki/Homozygous" title="Homozygous" class="mw-redirect">homozygous</a> animal.</p> 
     <p>Further testing uses <a href="/wiki/PCR" title="PCR" class="mw-redirect">PCR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_hybridization" title="Southern hybridization" class="mw-redirect">Southern hybridization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequencing</a> is conducted to confirm that an organism contains the new gene. These tests can also confirm the chromosomal location and copy number of the inserted gene. The presence of the gene does not guarantee it will be <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">expressed</a> at appropriate levels in the target tissue so methods that look for and measure the gene products (RNA and protein) are also used. These include <a href="/wiki/Northern_hybridization" title="Northern hybridization" class="mw-redirect">northern hybridization</a>, quantitative <a href="/wiki/RT-PCR" title="RT-PCR" class="mw-redirect">RT-PCR</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_blot" title="Western blot">Western blot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immunofluorescence" title="Immunofluorescence">immunofluorescence</a>, <a href="/wiki/ELISA" title="ELISA">ELISA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotypic</a> analysis. For stable transformation the gene should be passed to the offspring in a <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">Mendelian inheritance</a> pattern, so the organism's offspring are also studied.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genome_editing">Genome editing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Genome editing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing" title="Genome editing">Genome editing</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Genome editing is a type of genetic engineering in which DNA is inserted, replaced, or removed from a <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> using artificially engineered <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nucleases</a>, or "molecular scissors." The nucleases create specific <a href="/wiki/Double-strand_breaks" title="Double-strand breaks" class="mw-redirect">double-stranded breaks</a> (DSBs) at desired locations in the genome, and harness the cell’s endogenous mechanisms to repair the induced break by natural processes of <a href="/wiki/Homologous_recombination" title="Homologous recombination">homologous recombination</a> (HR) and <a href="/wiki/Nonhomologous_end_joining" title="Nonhomologous end joining" class="mw-redirect">nonhomologous end-joining</a> (NHEJ). There are currently four families of engineered nucleases: <a href="/wiki/Meganuclease" title="Meganuclease">meganucleases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger_nuclease" title="Zinc finger nuclease">zinc finger nucleases</a> (ZFNs), <a href="/wiki/Transcription_activator-like_effector_nuclease" title="Transcription activator-like effector nuclease">transcription activator-like effector nucleases</a> (TALENs), and the Cas9-guideRNA system (adapted from the <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a> prokarotic immune system).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In contrast to artificial genome editing natural genome editing occurs through viral and sub-viral agents competent in identification of genetic syntax structures for insertion/deletion processes with the result of conserved selection processes.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <p>Genetic engineering has applications in medicine, research, industry and agriculture and can be used on a wide range of plants, animals and micro organisms.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>In medicine, genetic engineering has been used in manufacturing drugs, to create model animals and do laboratory research, and in <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a>.</p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Manufacturing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_fermentation" title="Industrial fermentation">Industrial fermentation</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Genetic engineering is used to mass-produce insulin, human growth hormones, <a href="/wiki/Follistim" title="Follistim" class="mw-redirect">follistim</a> (for treating infertility), <a href="/wiki/Albumin_human" title="Albumin human" class="mw-redirect">human albumin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibodies" title="Monoclonal antibodies" class="mw-redirect">monoclonal antibodies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antihemophilic_factor" title="Antihemophilic factor" class="mw-redirect">antihemophilic factors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> and many other drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Mouse <a href="/wiki/Hybridoma_technology" title="Hybridoma technology">hybridomas</a>, cells fused together to create <a href="/wiki/Monoclonal_antibodies" title="Monoclonal antibodies" class="mw-redirect">monoclonal antibodies</a>, have been humanised through genetic engineering to create human monoclonal antibodies.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Genetically engineered viruses are being developed that can still confer immunity, but lack the <a href="/wiki/Infectious_Disease" title="Infectious Disease" class="mw-redirect">infectious</a> <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequence" title="DNA sequence" class="mw-redirect">sequences</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">Genetically modified organism</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Genetic engineering is used to create <a href="/wiki/Animal_models" title="Animal models" class="mw-redirect">animal models</a> of human diseases. <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_mouse" title="Genetically modified mouse">Genetically modified mice</a> are the most common genetically engineered animal model.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> They have been used to study and model cancer (the <a href="/wiki/Oncomouse" title="Oncomouse">oncomouse</a>), obesity, heart disease, diabetes, arthritis, substance abuse, anxiety, aging and Parkinson disease.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Potential cures can be tested against these mouse models. Also genetically modified pigs have been bred with the aim of increasing the success of <a href="/wiki/Xenotransplantation" title="Xenotransplantation">pig to human organ transplantation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Medical_News_Today_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medical_News_Today-78">[78]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gene_therapy">Gene therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gene therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">Gene therapy</a>
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">Gene therapy</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_engineering" title="Human genetic engineering" class="mw-redirect">genetic engineering of humans</a>, generally by replacing defective genes with effective ones. This can occur in <a href="/wiki/Somatic_(biology)" title="Somatic (biology)">somatic</a> tissue or <a href="/wiki/Germline" title="Germline">germline</a> tissue.</p> 
     <p>Somatic gene therapy has been studied in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_research" title="Clinical research">clinical research</a> in several diseases, including <a href="/wiki/X-linked_severe_combined_immunodeficiency" title="X-linked severe combined immunodeficiency">X-linked SCID</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chronic_lymphocytic_leukemia" title="Chronic lymphocytic leukemia" class="mw-redirect">chronic lymphocytic leukemia</a> (CLL),<sup id="cite_ref-Porter_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Glybera" title="Glybera" class="mw-redirect">Glybera</a> became the first gene therapy treatment to be approved for clinical use in either Europe or the United States after its endorsement by the European Commission.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallagher_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Richards2012_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Richards2012-83">[83]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>With regard to <a href="/wiki/Germline" title="Germline">germline</a> gene therapy, the scientific community has been opposed to attempts to alter genes in humans in inheritable ways using <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> since the technology was first introduced,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and the caution has continued as the technology has progressed.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> With the advent of new techniques like <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>, in March 2015 scientists urged a worldwide ban on clinical use of gene editing technologies to edit the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> in a way that can be inherited.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20150319_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150319-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20150303-AP_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150303-AP-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SCI-20150319_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20150319-88">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NAT-20150312_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20150312-89">[89]</a></sup> In April 2015, Chinese researchers sparked controversy when they <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Society_and_culture" title="CRISPR">reported</a> results of <a href="/wiki/Basic_research" title="Basic research">basic research</a> experiments in which they edited the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> of non-viable <a href="/wiki/Human_embryos" title="Human embryos" class="mw-redirect">human embryos</a> using <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20150423_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150423-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PC-20150418_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC-20150418-91">[91]</a></sup> In December 2015, scientists of major world academies called for a moratorium on inheritable <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> edits, including those related to <a href="/wiki/Gene_drive#CRISPR.2FCas9" title="Gene drive">CRISPR-Cas9</a> technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20151203-nw_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151203-nw-92">[92]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>There are also ethical concerns should the technology be used not just for treatment, but for enhancement, modification or alteration of a human beings' appearance, adaptability, intelligence, character or behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The distinction between cure and enhancement can also be difficult to establish.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Transhumanism" title="Transhumanism">Transhumanists</a> consider the enhancement of humans desirable.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_2">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:PCWmice1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/PCWmice1.jpg" width="200" height="97" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="97"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:PCWmice1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Knockout_mouse" title="Knockout mouse">Knockout mice</a>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png/220px-Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png/330px-Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png/440px-Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="600"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Expression_of_Human_Wild-Type_and_P239S_Mutant_Palladin.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Human cells in which some proteins are fused with 
        <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">green fluorescent protein</a> to allow them to be visualised
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Genetic engineering is an important tool for <a href="/wiki/Natural_scientists" title="Natural scientists" class="mw-redirect">natural scientists</a>. Genes and other genetic information from a wide range of organisms are transformed into bacteria for storage and modification, creating <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_bacteria" title="Genetically modified bacteria">genetically modified bacteria</a> in the process. Bacteria are cheap, easy to grow, <a href="/wiki/Clone_(cell_biology)" title="Clone (cell biology)">clonal</a>, multiply quickly, relatively easy to transform and can be stored at -80&nbsp;°C almost indefinitely. Once a gene is isolated it can be stored inside the bacteria providing an unlimited supply for research.</p> 
     <p>Organisms are genetically engineered to discover the functions of certain genes. This could be the effect on the phenotype of the organism, where the gene is expressed or what other genes it interacts with. These experiments generally involve loss of function, gain of function, tracking and expression.</p> 
     <ul> 
      <li><b>Loss of function experiments</b>, such as in a <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">gene knockout</a> experiment, in which an organism is engineered to lack the activity of one or more genes. A knockout experiment involves the creation and manipulation of a DNA construct <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i>, which, in a simple knockout, consists of a copy of the desired gene, which has been altered such that it is non-functional. <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cells" title="Embryonic stem cells" class="mw-redirect">Embryonic stem cells</a> incorporate the altered gene, which replaces the already present functional copy. These stem cells are injected into <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">blastocysts</a>, which are implanted into surrogate mothers. This allows the experimenter to analyze the defects caused by this <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutation</a> and thereby determine the role of particular genes. It is used especially frequently in <a href="/wiki/Developmental_biology" title="Developmental biology">developmental biology</a>. Another method, useful in organisms such as <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a> (fruit fly), is to induce mutations in a large population and then screen the progeny for the desired mutation. A similar process can be used in both plants and <a href="/wiki/Prokaryotes" title="Prokaryotes" class="mw-redirect">prokaryotes</a>. Loss of function tells whether or not a protein is required for a function, but it does not always mean it's sufficient, especially if a function requires multiple proteins and lose the said function if one protein is missing.</li> 
      <li><b>Gain of function experiments</b>, the logical counterpart of knockouts. These are sometimes performed in conjunction with knockout experiments to more finely establish the function of the desired gene. The process is much the same as that in knockout engineering, except that the construct is designed to increase the function of the gene, usually by providing extra copies of the gene or inducing synthesis of the protein more frequently. Gain of function is used to tell whether or not a protein is sufficient for a function, but it does not always mean it's required. Especially when dealing with genetic/functional redundancy.</li> 
      <li><b>Tracking experiments</b>, which seek to gain information about the localization and interaction of the desired protein. One way to do this is to replace the wild-type gene with a 'fusion' gene, which is a juxtaposition of the wild-type gene with a reporting element such as <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">green fluorescent protein</a> (GFP) that will allow easy visualization of the products of the genetic modification. While this is a useful technique, the manipulation can destroy the function of the gene, creating secondary effects and possibly calling into question the results of the experiment. More sophisticated techniques are now in development that can track protein products without mitigating their function, such as the addition of small sequences that will serve as binding motifs to monoclonal antibodies.</li> 
      <li><b>Expression studies</b> aim to discover where and when specific proteins are produced. In these experiments, the DNA sequence before the DNA that codes for a protein, known as a gene's <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(biology)" title="Promoter (biology)" class="mw-redirect">promoter</a>, is reintroduced into an organism with the protein coding region replaced by a reporter gene such as GFP or an enzyme that catalyzes the production of a dye. Thus the time and place where a particular protein is produced can be observed. Expression studies can be taken a step further by altering the promoter to find which pieces are crucial for the proper expression of the gene and are actually bound by transcription factor proteins; this process is known as <a href="/wiki/Promoter_bashing" title="Promoter bashing">promoter bashing</a>.</li> 
     </ul> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Using genetic engineering techniques one can transform microorganisms such as bacteria or yeast, or transform cells from multicellular organisms such as insects or mammals, with a gene coding for a useful protein, such as an enzyme, so that the transformed organism will <a href="/wiki/Protein_expression_(biotechnology)" title="Protein expression (biotechnology)" class="mw-redirect">overexpress</a> the desired protein. One can manufacture mass quantities of the protein by growing the transformed organism in <a href="/wiki/Bioreactor" title="Bioreactor">bioreactor</a> equipment using techniques of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_fermentation" title="Industrial fermentation">industrial fermentation</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Protein_purification" title="Protein purification">purifying</a> the protein.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Some genes do not work well in bacteria, so yeast, insect cells, or mammalians cells, each a <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryote</a>, can also be used.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> These techniques are used to produce medicines such as <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_growth_hormone" title="Human growth hormone" class="mw-redirect">human growth hormone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>, supplements such as <a href="/wiki/Tryptophan" title="Tryptophan">tryptophan</a>, aid in the production of food (<a href="/wiki/Chymosin" title="Chymosin">chymosin</a> in cheese making) and fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Other applications involving genetically engineered bacteria being investigated involve making the bacteria perform tasks outside their natural cycle, such as making <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> cleaning up oil spills, carbon and other toxic waste<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and detecting arsenic in drinking water.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Certain genetically modified microbes can also be used in <a href="/wiki/Biomining" title="Biomining">biomining</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a>, due to their ability to extract heavy metals from their environment and incorporate them into compounds that are more easily recoverable.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p> 
     <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental.2C_lab_scale_industrial_applications">Experimental, lab scale industrial applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Experimental, lab scale industrial applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
     <p>In <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_virus" title="Genetically modified virus">genetically modified virus</a> has been used in an academic lab as a scaffold for assembling a more environmentally friendly lithium-ion battery.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Bacteria have been engineered to function as sensors by expressing a fluorescent protein under certain environmental conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Bt_plants.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/220px-Bt_plants.png" width="220" height="401" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/330px-Bt_plants.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Bt_plants.png/440px-Bt_plants.png 2x" data-file-width="715" data-file-height="1302"></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Bt_plants.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
        </div> Bt-toxins present in 
        <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a> leaves (bottom image) protect it from extensive damage caused by 
        <a href="/wiki/European_corn_borer" title="European corn borer">European corn borer</a> 
        <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> (top image).
        <sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>One of the best-known and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">controversial</a> applications of genetic engineering is the creation and use of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a> or <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_fish" title="Genetically modified fish">genetically modified fish</a>, which are used to produce <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a> and materials with diverse uses. There are four main goals in generating genetically modified crops.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>One goal, and the first to be realized commercially, is to provide protection from environmental threats, such as cold (in the case of <a href="/wiki/Ice-minus_bacteria" title="Ice-minus bacteria">Ice-minus bacteria</a>), or pathogens, such as insects or viruses, and/or resistance to <a href="/wiki/Herbicides" title="Herbicides" class="mw-redirect">herbicides</a>. There are also fungal and virus resistant crops developed or in development.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> They have been developed to make the insect and weed management of crops easier and can indirectly increase crop yield.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another goal in generating GMOs is to modify the quality of produce by, for instance, increasing the nutritional value or providing more industrially useful qualities or quantities.<sup id="cite_ref-Deborah_B._Whitman_2000_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deborah_B._Whitman_2000-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Amflora" title="Amflora">Amflora</a> potato, for example, produces a more industrially useful blend of starches. Cows have been engineered to produce more protein in their milk to facilitate cheese production.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Soybeans and canola have been genetically modified to produce more healthy oils.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another goal consists of driving the GMO to produce materials that it does not normally make. One example is <a href="/wiki/Pharming_(genetics)" title="Pharming (genetics)">"pharming"</a>, which uses crops as <a href="/wiki/Bioreactors" title="Bioreactors" class="mw-redirect">bioreactors</a> to produce vaccines, drug intermediates, or drug themselves; the useful product is purified from the harvest and then used in the standard pharmaceutical production process.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Cows and goats have been engineered to express drugs and other proteins in their milk, and in 2009 the FDA approved a drug produced in goat milk.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Another goal in generating GMOs, is to directly improve yield by accelerating growth, or making the organism more hardy (for plants, by improving salt, cold or drought tolerance).<sup id="cite_ref-Deborah_B._Whitman_2000_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deborah_B._Whitman_2000-110">[110]</a></sup> Some agriculturally important animals have been genetically modified with growth hormones to increase their size.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>The genetic engineering of agricultural crops can increase the growth rates and resistance to different diseases caused by <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite" class="mw-redirect">parasites</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity-118">[118]</a></sup> This is beneficial as it can greatly increase the production of food sources with the usage of fewer resources that would be required to host the world's growing populations. These modified crops would also reduce the usage of chemicals, such as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a>, and therefore decrease the severity and frequency of the damages produced by these chemical <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Biodiversity_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Biodiversity-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20120613Guardian_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20120613Guardian-119">[119]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Ethical and safety concerns have been raised around the use of genetically modified food.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> A major safety concern relates to the human health implications of eating genetically modified food, in particular whether toxic or allergic reactions could occur.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genetic_pollution" title="Genetic pollution">Gene flow</a> into related non-transgenic crops, off target effects on <a href="/wiki/Beneficial_insects" title="Beneficial insects">beneficial organisms</a> and the impact on <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> are important environmental issues.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Ethical concerns involve <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_on_genetically_modified_foods" title="Religious views on genetically modified foods">religious issues</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporate</a> control of the food supply, <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> rights and the level of labeling needed on genetically modified products.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="BioArt_and_entertainment">BioArt and entertainment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: BioArt and entertainment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
     <p>Genetic engineering is also being used to create <a href="/wiki/BioArt" title="BioArt">BioArt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Some bacteria have been genetically engineered to create black and white photographs.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p> 
     <p>Genetic engineering has also been used to create novelty items such as lavender-colored <a href="/wiki/Dianthus_caryophyllus#Colors" title="Dianthus caryophyllus">carnations</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-physorg_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physorg-125">[125]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blue_rose" title="Blue rose">blue roses</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/GloFish" title="GloFish">glowing fish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_genetic_engineering" title="Regulation of genetic engineering">Regulation of genetic engineering</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_the_release_of_genetically_modified_organisms" title="Regulation of the release of genetically modified organisms">Regulation of the release of genetically modified organisms</a>
     </div> 
     <p>The regulation of genetic engineering concerns the approaches taken by governments to assess and manage the risks associated with the development and release of genetically modified crops. There are differences in the regulation of GM crops between countries, with some of the most marked differences occurring between the USA and Europe. Regulation varies in a given country depending on the intended use of the products of the genetic engineering. For example, a crop not intended for food use is generally not reviewed by authorities responsible for food safety. Starting in the late 1980s, guidance on assessing the safety of genetically engineered plants and food emerged from organizations including the FAO and WHO.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy">Controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genetic_engineering&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
     <div role="note" class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles: 
      <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">Genetically modified food controversies</a> and 
      <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_engineering" title="Human genetic engineering" class="mw-redirect">Human genetic engineering</a>
     </div> 
     <p>Critics have objected to use of genetic engineering per se on several grounds, including ethical concerns, ecological concerns, and economic concerns raised by the fact GM techniques and GM organisms are subject to intellectual property law. GMOs also are involved in controversies over GM food with respect to whether food produced from GM crops is safe, whether it should be labeled, and whether GM crops are needed to address the world's food needs. See the <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">genetically modified food controversies</a> article for discussion of issues about GM crops and GM food. These controversies have led to litigation, international trade disputes, and protests, and to restrictive regulation of commercial products in some countries.</p>
